One indication that he is autistic is that he has difficulties continuing a conversation even after the grueling process of starting a conversation with someone. On more than one occasion, people tried to start a conversation with him, but he just cannot talk to these people.  Also, Christopher shows a need for sameness and routines. The way in which he counts cars passing by while on the school bus to know what kind of a day it will be. Another example of his rituals is that he will have absolutely nothing to do with his food if the different foods on his plate touch each other.<br /><br />This book definitely shows you what it would be like, how you would think, and just how functioning in every day life as an autistic person would be like. B000HWZ4A2|2.0|As protagonist Howard Roark, Gary Cooper brings all the fire and passion of a used teabag. Patricia Neal plays love-interest place holder Dominique Francon who falls in love with Howard because she apparently finds his \\"The rest of the world can suck it\\" philosophy endearing. Practically all of the rest of the characters fall into two distinct categories, those who respect Howard but just can't live up to his standards or those who hate Howard because they can't live up to his standards. It's a very black and white story (no pun intended) with no gray areas. Like John Galt, Howard Roark is a mystery to lesser men. The mystery to me is how anyone consider this an admirable, much less realistic, mentality. Roark isn't principled, he's a diva. Flat broke, he turns down jobs because he only wants to do what HE wants to do. Imagine having that conversation with a physician:<br /><br />You: Doctor, I've got this weird growth and it keeps getting bigger.<br />Doctor: Oh, sorry. I don't do growths. My only passion in life is treating rashes with topical creams.<br />You: But I'm really worried. What if it's cancer? Can't you run some tests?<br />Doctor: Nope, sorry. That would be compromising my principles. Why would you ask me to do that? Come back when you have a rash.<br /><br />It isn't a story about principles, it's about a grown man with the mentality of a spoiled child who wins in the end only because he's a character in a book. Holding oneself to a high standard is admirable. So is pursuing a dream. But in the real world necessity and practicality are also (major) considerations. There's no shame in doing a day's work for a day's pay. It doesn't make you sheep, it makes you a responsible adult. I think Rand's message is that if everyone is serving their own best interests they will give 100% and society will benefit as a consequence. But most people don't want to pay you to follow YOUR dream or achieve YOUR vision. They want to pay you to provide a good or service that matters to THEM. Doing so isn't a compromise of principles, it's just how society works.

B008OTTRXU|5.0|Five years ago, I was watching the fifth season of Canadian Idol. That was Carly Rae Jepsen's year. I remember her as a quirky singer-songwriter type--not my personal preference when it comes to genre or style, but charismatic nonetheless. Who would have thought, half a decade later, that she would have reinvented herself as a pop princess and taken over the world with what has to be the most well-known pop song of the year? Now, she's released her first big pop album. No, not just \\"pop.\\" It's bubblegum pop, through and through. So how does it rate?<br /><br />1. Tiny Little Bows - Opens with a sample of Sam Cooke's Cupid, which is an oddly old-school choice, before bounding into a string-laden, intensely propulsive pop song. It's like Call Me Maybe part two. The verses are the standouts here. 9/10<br /><br />2. This Kiss - The third single, and probably the most 80's inspired moment on the album. It's one of those top-down, drive-around-in-the-summer pop songs with a huge chorus and even better verses. Still, it doesn't quite have the same sticking power as her very best material. 9/10<br /><br />3. Call Me Maybe - There's a reason this has been so huge around the world. Sure, it sounds a bit tired now, having been played to death (and then some) on the radio, tv and youtube, but think back to the months when it first came out and how refreshingly upbeat it was. Give it time, it'll sound great again. 10/10<br /><br />4. Curiosity - This was one of my favorite tracks on her previous EP and, as a pop song, it's still amazing. But, it's getting downgraded in score for the hollow production revamp its been given on the album version. It sounded so much better with its warmer, slightly more organic sound. The production drains something, here. 9/10<br /><br />5. Good Time (with Owl City) - I'm not a fan of Owl City. At all. So, it surprised me how much this single won me over in the end. It's still unbelievably glossy and poppy, and the chorus is instantly catchy, but it's a slight step down from the very strong opening segment of the album. 8/10<br /><br />7. Turn Me Up - Now this is what I'm talking about! This is total, pop/dance bliss. The propulsive production and aggressive melody really makes this a standout, despite its boring title. 10/10<br /><br />8. Hurt So Good - This is a little all over the place. It's got some great power keyboard riffs that are straight from the 80's, but it's a little frantic--particularly the chorus. 8/10<br /><br />9. Beautiful (ft. Justin Bieber) - The only slower (and even then, its midtempo) track on the album. It's nothing special, but it serves as a much-needed breather from all the high energy bubblegum that preceded it. Of anything on the album, it sounds the most like her older stuff. 8/10<br /><br />10. Tonight I'm Getting Over You - A big, contemporary club banger. I believe this is the next single in Canada, and I can see why. It's not the strongest track on the album, but it's one of the most current. 9/10<br /><br />11. Guitar String / Wedding Ring - Despite the cringeworthy title, this is a definite standout. Impossibly catchy, it's got a lot of character for what's essentially a straightforward pop track. I love the dizzying synths in the chorus and the unexpected routes she leads the melody. 10/10<br /><br />12. Your Heart Is A Muscle - This is pure 90's, lighters-in-the-air cheese. B009AMABH6|5.0|*Contains Spoilers*<br /><br />I read this book when Robert Pattinson signed on to the project, and to be honest, I didn't like it on the first reading. It grew on me by the second reading, but the movie made me love it. One of the challenges Cosmopolis faces is that it's impossible complete the phrase, \\"The moral of the story is....\\" and so many people, me included, dislike things that can't be easily defined.  I've seen interviews with David Cronenberg and Robert Pattinson where they say that it is impossible to tell you what they story is about, but don't make the mistake of thinking that it's about nothing, it's just about open to so many interpretations that it's impossible to pin down.  I'll give you mine, but rest assured that if you don't like what I see, chances are you'll see something else entirely anyway.<br /><br />The story, in it's most basic form, is about Eric Packer's limo ride across the crowded city to get a haircut.  He encounters various people and scenarios along the way, and inexpicably he encounters his new wife at several stops along the way, even though that would seem to be impossible since he's seemingly moving forward (albeit slowly) and she appears to intend to remain somewhat stationary. In a sense I think the limo ride is a microcosm for a Eric's journey through life.  There is that saying that some people are in your life for a reason and some people are in your life for a season and I think he encounters his wife several times as a symbol of all people in a person's life that they keep coming back to, but it's poignant because he never really understands her, or her him even though there is that desire. They both very much are pretending to be normal, or at least understand normal, and don't we all feel that way sometimes?  Even if he doesn't realize it the limo can be seen as his hearse because life is, afterall, a journey toward death.  It's important to note that Eric is going to all this trouble of traveling across town to see his father's barber, which I think is symbolic of Eric's desire to somehow either connect with his past, become like or understand his father, or reach some sort of arbitrary life goal he's set for himself based on familial expectations all while he's sprucing himself up for his inevitable demise.<br /><br />One of the few misses in this movie is that Cronenberg left out a scene where Eric encounters \\"three hundred naked people sprawled in the street\\" and he takes off his clothes and joins them and even thinks he sees his wife, Elise, in the crowd.  In the book, I thought this was the only time that Eric really seemed to fit in with the people around him and it seemed to be the first time he understood Elise.  It seemed like a sort of ritualistic cleansing before burial for Eric.  Cronenberg also leaves out that in addition to Eric losing all his money he also intentionally loses all of Elise's as well.  I think this points to the \\"you can't take it with you\\" philosophy and helps Eric resign himself to the fact that he's done.  That Cronenberg didn't focus on these two bits of information makes me think that my entire interpretation of the story is wrong and you all should go about your day.  Nothing to see here folks.<br /><br />In a much hyped about scene worth the price of admission, Eric Packer encounters Benno Levin, his \\"stalker\\" for lack of a better word, played by Paul Giamatti. The fact that Eric Packer's security team has been working locating an undetermined \\"threat\\" to Eric's safety which was presumably Levin, but that Eric inexplicably stumbles upon the warehouse where he is hiding with no real reason for going there in the first place calls for a similar suspension of disbelief to the one that allows you to believe that Eric can repeatedly and coincidentally encounter Elise when he is traveling and she, seemingly, is not.  It brought to mind that moment in Fight Club when you learn that Edward Norton IS Tyler Durden.  Imagining that that scene might betaking place inside Eric's head kinda blows my mind.  That being said, I'm pretty sure I'm wrong.<br /><br />This movie is worth being seen because it is one of very few thinking movies. Much has been made about the fact that the timing of this movie coincides nicely with the Occupy Wallstreet movement but even though there are so many aspects of this movie that can make you think about the validity (or invalidity) of the financial market, this movie is really just about one person trying to find meaning in life.taking place inside Eric's head kinda blows my mind.  That being said, I'm pretty sure I'm wrong.<br /><br />This movie is worth being seen because it is one of very few thinking movies. B008I3R2VG|3.0|Battle Born is not a Killers album; it is another Brandon Flowers solo album--the sequel to Flamingo, if you will. It is vast, anthemic, incredibly polished, and lacking the beloved grit and angst of quintessential Killers sound a la Sam's Town. Having six producers on the album is a bit excessive and, in my opinion, too many cooks in the kitchen. The album is almost too mature. I feel like it didn't write itself, but rather, Flowers and Co. set out to accomplish a certain plethora of sound--steroidal cognizance of the end goal sabotaging the natural songwriting progression, which, in the process, negates a crucial organicity. Therein lies the inherent difference between Battle Born and The Killers' first few albums, their last phenomenal offering to date being Sawdust, its collection of B-sides and unreleased tracks from the Hot Fuss and Sam's Town era proving far more infectious than anything on Battle Born. There comes a point in every artist's career when they become too conscious of their work and try to do too much. This is okay. It's all part of the process. While Flowers' voice sounds unequivocally grand and on point, I'm still left with the pervasive feeling that any time spent listening to Battle Born could be spent listening to an edgier, more authentic Killers, e.g., \\"Bling\\" and \\"Uncle Johnny.\\" Nonetheless, I'm glad they're not trying to recreate the same formula. I'm glad they're arguably overthinking things and releasing an enjoyable work that's a fully realized triumph with respect to its intent, yet a concurrent misstep. Oftentimes a Battle Born is rendered before another masterpiece is furnished. 3.5 stars.

0525952926|5.0|After 34 years of gripping historical thrillers, Ken Follett is back with the second installment of his \\"Century Trilogy.\\"  Perhaps nothing could surpass [[ASIN:0451232852 Fall of Giants]] (indeed, I'll concede this just barely earns five stars, below the 5+ I'd give the first volume), but on its own this still earns a heartfelt \\"loved it!\\" from me.  Follett has a gift for shining the light on historic moments through an array of intimate moments. This is the story of World War Two, and like the war itself, the book is driven by the courage (and mistakes) of individual people.  This book starts in 1933, following the dark threads of discontent around the globe, weaving together stories in ways both unexpected and inevitable -- in the Pacific, in Nazi Germany, among the American and European allies, and of course the adolescent USSR.  This is a true epic in the best of the tradition: born in the very guts of its struggling characters, it shows us how the Great War (of Volume 1) and its fallout - repairing mistakes, building on failures, expanding hopes -- led the next generation to catastrophe, aspiration and destruction on an ever-grander scale.<br /><br />As with many trans-generational sequels, the first book's characters now retire to the background while their children take center stage.  The main character is Lloyd Williams, who as a toddler ascended the steps of Parliament at the end of Fall of Giants (son of Welsh maid turned London activist Ethel and her former employer, the Earl Fitzherbert). Lloyd is now a young man, heir to his mother's headstrong conviction if not his father's noble pride.  In Germany, Carla von Ulrich (daughter of the last books passionate lovers, Lady Maud Fitzherbert and Walter von Ulrich) comes of age with the rising of the Third Reich. The heirs to American Idealist Gus Dewar are Woody and Chuck, off to the west and east to fight in the Pacific.  And from the last book's Russian orphan brothers Lev and Gregori, we have young Daisy Peshkov (a deeply drawn picture of an American Mob Princess breaking out of her preconceived limitations of sexual and political power), and her cousin Volodya Peshkov, fighting to keep his legs under him in post-revolutionary Russia. Since this is fiction, Follett does indulge in liberal use of coincidence to bring characters together -- but that's a worthwhile contrivance.  Because it's through growing to know (if not love) these families that we can feel how this global drama swept so many lives up in its wake. In essence, if not in actuality, this is the story of our parents and grandparents -- struggling with the wars fought, diplomacy wrought and atrocities rendered all around them.  It's a gift of great fiction to bring their stories alive before they fade forever into the land of textbooks.<br /><br />While such a huge plot could have run wild, Follett is selective in what events he covers (which may disappoint some) in order to stay close his core of characters.  The book covers 16 years of this dark era in history, where people on every level of society faced the gripping uncertainty about the very future of the world . And by 1949, as the winter breaks but the \\"Cold Peace\\" settles over Europe, we can feel the relief and apprehension -- broken but surviving families, the fragile peace of exhausted nations. The story of World War II is complete, but the stage is set for the century's next haunting chapter.<br /><br />I've loved Ken Follett since I first read [[ASIN:0062020897 Eye of the Needle]],  and I breathe a sigh of relief with each book that he still approaches each project with a love for storytelling and meticulous craftsmanship. I will note that, like his other works, this book is sexually racy with violence that, sadly, is necessary to justly depict the horror of the times (so take care recommending it with teens for instance ).  For me, I'll be holding my breath for two more years, when - can't believe I'm saying this -- I can finally learn all Ican about the Cold War.

B008J34466|4.0|Pink is a swirling firecracker of fun, fizzy contradictions - at least if her musical output portrays her accurately - and \\"The Truth About Love\\" lives up to her hit-heavy track record.<br /><br />Unlike 2008's \\"Funhouse,\\" which was more a grab bag of singles with solid album tracks in-between to maintain the flow, \\"The Truth About Love\\" is less focused and more sprawling, which is surely what Pink intended. 1401322786|5.0|We all have moments in life where we wished we could have gone back in time, fix the mistakes, right the wrongs, make more time for those we care about, wish we had more time to meet deadlines, etc. Then we realize that all this time has gone by and we haven't done a thing with the time we have now, because we are too preoccupied with the time that hasn't arrived, or thought so much about what lies ahead that we forget about the people and surroundings we have around us now.<br /><br />Reading about Victor and Sarah, and the creation of Father Time, had me hooked right from the very start. Like Dor, we are all preoccupied with the measurement of things because it seems to give us certain meaning or understanding of our lives, and then we forget we are putting too much importance on the measurement of time, rather than the quality of time. Victor and Sarah in my opinion embody what we either strive to preserve, or put too much effort to want when we really need to focus on the NOW in our lives.<br /><br />For me personally, I have hit a point in my personal life where I have been consumed with wondering what I could change if I could go back in time, fix all the years of broken relationships, heartache, time apart from my kids, etc. This book helped me to realize that I can't change that, and I can't try to manipulate my future, I just need to focus on making this life right now the best time I could ever possibly have. Thank you Mitch. You wrote another gem.
B008966SIU|5.0|Four albums into their career, Grizzly Bear are no longer developing into one of the best bands of our generation. They've already arrived. The Brooklyn indie folk rockers utilized eclectic instrumentation and heart touching harmonies to set forth a body of work that became the envy of many bands. But they apparently aren't content, as Shields focuses on pushing their boundaries still further.<br /><br />Their sophomore release Yellow House established the band's core sound; this is where they became known for their dreamy, pastoral chamber pop. However, it was their third album, Veckatimest, that would serve as a key turning point for the band. It took their pre-existing ideas and harnessed them into a set of 12 concise and cohesive songs.<br /><br />It was the type of album that allowed its songs to simmer and rise, slowly going through several progressions until it reached an apex. Once those songs reached their sweet spot they lingered there, allowing you to drink in the full splendor and majesty of their most creative moments. Veckatimest was by no means a restrained album, but if there was one weakness it was perhaps that they tended to rely on that formula a little much. Shields is even more adventurous than its predecessor by not only varying their formula, but by also pushing their songwriting and musicianship to yet another level.<br /><br />The first four tracks offer about as good of an opening salvo as you're ever like to hope for. Lead single \\"Sleeping Ute\\" tries to do what \\"Southern Point\\" did on Veckatimest -- quickly blow you away with Daniel Rossen's dizzying instrumentation and mellow croon. And again they succeed without a shadow of a doubt. Rossen's lead guitar is heavily nuanced and stylized, even dipping into a little bit of heavy rock. And there's some minor tinges of psychedelia if that isn't enough for you.<br /><br />\\"Speak in Rounds\\" focuses much more on vocals while never letting up from the opening bombast of \\"Sleeping Ute.\\" The instrumentation is more subdued, but the melodies make it feel organic, living, breathing, moving, and exciting. It even gets a little bit brassy near the end.<br /><br />Following that is something Grizzly Bear have never done before: a hazy, fully ambient little keyboard track which briefly washes over you with minor xylophone flourishes. Their early work has sometimes floated in ambiance, but it's never been as fully realized as it is on \\"Adelma.\\" It serves as a breather after that dynamic one-two opening punch.<br /><br />Had enough rest? Good, because now it's time the album's second pre-release track, \\"Yet Again.\\" Vocal harmonies have always been one of Grizzly Bear's cornerstones, and this track is the first great representation of that on Shields. It sees Droste getting a bit wistful and moody; he appears to not quite be content with his place in the world but is trying to come to terms with it. It's supplemented with haunting harmonies and psychedelia, and near the end we get something we don't hear much from Grizzly Bear -- propulsive drumming from Christopher Bear that helps drive Droste's message home.<br /><br />From here, the band begins to relax a bit and settle into a groove. The instrumentation tends to take a backseat to allow the singers' voice to shine. The next best track is \\"Gun Shy\\" which is Shields's true showcase piece in terms of harmony and vocal arrangement. Although they've always been strong in this department, the competing interplay between Droste and Chris Taylor on the main hook is truly the stuff of legends.<br /><br />Elsewhere, the eerie strings and minimalist nature of \\"The Hunt\\" sees Grizzly Bear making an effort to update their sound from the Yellow House/Horn of Plenty era. Meanwhile, \\"Half Gate\\" serves as another reminder of how powerful drumming is shifting the band's sound.<br /><br />Lyrically, they have a tendency to focus on loneliness and isolation. A cursory glance through their lyric booklet might lead an inattentive readerto conclude that many of their themes are about relationships. A better way to put it is that they concern relationships, but aren't about them specifically.<br /><br />Rather, they tend to focus on a particular nuance in the breakdown of relations between two people. \\"What's Wrong,\\" for example, speaks of a relationship that falls apart because one person decides to sequester and close themselves off. \\"The Hunt,\\" meanwhile, talks about being addicted to the feelings of trying to piece together a crumbling relationship.<br /><br />Shields wraps up with Grizzly Bear's opus, \\"Sun in Your Eyes.\\" It starts off sleepily before lifting you up with a big, powerful chorus that is full of brass and life. It builds up like a post rock track, slowly building to a fever pitch until it unleashes its major crescendo highlighted by powerful cymbal clashes, weighty keys, dramatic harmonies, and the impassioned voice of Daniel Rossen. \\"I'm never coming back,\\" he cautions, as he fades out for the last time.<br /><br />Shields is a stunning achievement that transcends the boundaries of folk or any other genre tag you care to throw out. But one caveat is that the second half of the album is certainly more subdued and takes greater effort to get into. Because of the ambition of the first four song, it feels like the rest of the album is playing catchup. It has a hard time living up to the standard established there. For this reason, Veckatimest feels a nudge more consistent than Shields, although Shields reaches greater heights. Nonetheless, Grizzly Bear is proving themselves to be much more than we ever thought they could be - and that's truly saying something.

B001JI72AI|5.0|When oxygen cylinders violently outgassed aboard the ill-fated Apollo 13, one of the most dramatic events in space exploration history began to unfold. The entire world was glued to their TV sets as the story evolved, and what a story it was.<br /><br />Apollo 13 tells the story of this amazing rescue. It shows with great realism and detail exactly what happened and what steps the astronauts had to take to ensure their safe return. Over 5000 people on the ground were eventually enlisted in this effort, and if it hadn't been for them, the astronauts would never have made it home.<br /><br />A lot of effort went into recreating the look of the original launch command center, the lunar module and capsule, and Houston control. Former NASA experts were recruited to help with this, and during shooting in the command centers former NASA employees coached the actors on what to say and do. Toward the end of the movie, Jim Lovell makes a brief cameo as the Admiral aboard the U.S.S. Iwo Jima, which picked up the capsule, who congratulates Tom Hanks on their safe return.<br /><br />To recreate the weightless scenes, a cargo plane carrying the actors made 612 dives, collecting about 50 minutes of footage for the movie.<br /><br />Once the original accident occurred, the drama and tension is just non-stop until the very end. The astronauts battled deficiencies in oxygen, fuel cell/electrical power, increasing cold (it finally got to 34 degrees in the cabin), a nearly fatal increase in carbon dioxide (which was successfully averted). Astronaut Haise had a fever of 102 degrees from a urinary tract infection,  but still managed to pull his own weight during the long recovery process.<br /><br />I watched this movie just recently after seeing it for the first time in 1995, and it holds up very well. At this time Tom Hanks was coming off of back to back Academy Awards for Forrest Gump and Philadelphia, and he was arguably the hottest actor in the world at the time. But he followed it up with another great performance here. The rest of the cast is great as well, especially Ed Harris as the flight director, Gary Sinise as Ken Mattingly, Tom Paxton as Fred Haise, Kevin Bacon as Jack Swigert, and Kathleen Quinlan as Marilyn Lovell. This is one movie that has stood the test of time.up with another great performance here. The rest of the cast is great as well, especially Ed Harris as the flight director, Gary Sinise as Ken Mattingly, Tom Paxton as Fred Haise, Kevin Bacon as Jack Swigert, and Kathleen Quinlan as Marilyn Lovell. This is one movie that has stood the test of time.

B0030Y11XS|5.0|Michael Moore's Capitalism: A Love Story is quite a documentary! Although I don't agree with every last thing Moore believes, I have to admire his ability to make a documentary that is entertaining, poignant at times and informative (well, informative from his point of view). Moore's general premise, as you might know by now, is that capitalism stinks and he desperately tries to prove that all throughout the movie, stopping at practically nothing to make his point. For example, his interviews with priests are very well positioned in the film to influence his audience; and even if you don't agree with him I think the interviews with the priests are presented nicely. Moore also uses some pretty silly antics along the way; he wants to drive home his point in such a way that the audience won't forget his message anytime soon. Look for him to cordon off a bank building in downtown Manhattan as a crime scene!<br /><br />There's so much in this film that I scarcely know where to begin. For one, after the financial crash of 2008 Moore interviewed Congressmen and women who had to vote on a huge government bailout of big business with practically no time or advance notice whatsoever to review the bailout terms before voting. We learn that Congress initially voted against the bailout at least partly because it was unpopular with many constituents; and then Moore explores why the Congress was pressured by both political parties to vote again just days later, this time in favor of the bailout.<br /><br />One aspect of the film that definitely hit home for me was the topic of how greedy big banks routinely tricked people into taking out sub-prime mortgages on their homes without advising them of how the interest rates go dramatically skyrocket after an initial period of time; and the footage we get of the struggle one family in Miami, Florida goes through to keep their home was poignant. The segment about airline pilots being paid so little saddened me; and it was interesting and even slightly amusing to see Moore trying to understand how derivatives work on Wall Street. There's also time devoted to a Chicago factory worker's strike when they were laid off without pay or severance. We also learn how the federal government was changed possibly forever by the influence of big business and Wall Street; this process mostly started during the Reagan administration.<br /><br />There's so much more; it may seem as if I've given it all away but I assure you that I haven't.<br /><br />The DVD comes with several bonus features which expand on Moore's views in this film.<br /><br />Overall, Capitalism: A Love Story was Michael Moore's way to call it as he sees it when the topic is capitalism. This is a film most people are going to either love or hate because Moore's opinions run all through the film and people will either agree with him or disagree with him. Of course, there will be a third group of people like myself who agree with most of what he says but not quite all of it.even slightly amusing to see Moore trying to understand how derivatives work on Wall Street. B008LZHA3G|4.0|Bob Dylan celebrates 2012 by releasing his 35th studio album, [[ASIN:B008LZHA3G Tempest]] via Columbia Records.  Dylan often takes heat for his nasally vocal style with some going as far to chastise the legendary singer/songwriter stating `he can't sing.' Regardless of varying opining on Dylan's vocal timbre, Dylan's songwriting is superb and liberating.  His unique vocal style and timbre only further complements his accomplishments as a fiery, revolutionary songwriter.  This ten song set, led by a fourteen minute penultimate title cut, further highlights Dylan's gifts and talents.<br /><br />\\"Duquesne Whistle\\" initiates things with electric guitar, which is characterized by a lovely timbre. Piano, upright bass, and eventually folk-rock drums buttress the blues harmonic progression. Dylan's vocals may be gruff, but his gruffness is endearing to his storytelling, particularly on this solid opener.<br /><br />\\"Soon After Midnight\\" proceeds, ceding little if no momentum. Sporting a country-rock sensibility, \\"Soon After Midnight\\" contrasts the excitability of the opener with a slower, six-eight feel and a more lush production.  While it may not have the `bite' of the opener, it still possesses that top-notch pen.<br /><br />\\"Narrow Way\\" eclipses \\"Soon After Midnight,\\" opting for quicker tempo and a classic rock sensibility. The biting, distorted electric guitars sound superb.  The best lyric, featured in the chorus is \\"If I can't work up to you/you'll surely have to work down to me someday...\\" At 7:28, length would be the sole quibble. That said and in justification, Dylan tends to compose lengthier songs, so it is expected.<br /><br />\\"Long and Wasted Years\\" contrasts the length of \\"Narrow Way\\" in favor of brevity, clocking in at under 4:00.  Bluesy and country, the country-soul sensibility suits Dylan perfectly.  Couple a more relaxed six-eight meter as well as signature licks/riffs throughout its course, and \\"Long And Wasted Years\\" is by no means a `waste' of anything.  Dylan's storytelling skills, handled in confessional manner here, are magnificent.  The timbres working in tandem with Dylan's pen equal sensational.<br /><br />\\"Pay In Blood\\" further `ups the ante,' with its `no-holds-bar' overt rock and roll sound. Vocally, Dylan's approach sells this cut.  Add hook-line \\"...I pay in blood, but not my own...\\" from the refrain, and you've got one of Dylan's most memorable cuts in recent times.  Again playing on minimalist riffs and ideas, the unity established here is genius.<br /><br />\\"Scarlet Town\\" has a difficult act to follow, but it manages well.  It is the first slower cut that resides in 4/4 time as opposed to Dylan's standard six feel.  The banjo is a fine touch, adding to the folk-rock element of this cut.  Full fruition of drums, bass, guitars, and strings is truly a `sight' to hear - `ear candy' if you will. \\"Scarlet Town\\" does not dare eclipse heavyweights like \\"Narrow Way\\" or \\"Pay In Blood,\\" but it is still riding on a `high' plane.<br /><br />\\"Early Roman Kings\\" gives even the best a `run.'  Unapologetically blues-driven in sound, \\"Early Roman\\" is so firmly planted in blues-rock that it could be considered utterly `clich&eacute;.'  Dylan's vocals are easily in the blues idiom, furthering the appeal of this non-anachronistic cut by the songwriter.  Add organ and harmonica to the mix, not to mention some hip, HIP lines (\\"I ain't dead yet, my bell still rings...\\") and Dylan sounds nowhere near 71 years of age.<br /><br />\\"Tin Angel\\" takes a slower road, employing more minimally-driven ideas. The production is excellent as always as is songwriting. At 9:05, it is a bit `too' long.  No longer than the Titanic based \\"Tempest,\\" compiled of 45 verses and clocking in just shy of 14:00.  It is a `juggernaut' for  sure, BUT Dylan does a great job.  His tone only further colors the lyrics detailing the ship and the folk-rock/country-infused production makes the duration bearable.  \\"Roll On John\\" shies away from double digitlength, clocking in still at over seven minutes.  As always, songwriting is credible and the overall track well done.  \\"Roll On John\\" loses some of its effectiveness due to the grandeur of the `elite' cuts and due to the exhaustive lengths of \\"Tin  Angel\\" and \\"Tempest\\" that arrive before it. Those issues aside, \\"Roll On John\\" is about as good/consistent as anything else.<br /><br />Rolling Stone awarded Tempest a lofty five-star rating.  While that may be a bit overwrought, it is not too far fetched as [[ASIN:B008LZHA3G Tempest]] is a fine album. There are no overt misses; only overindulgence in length and number of verses ever hurts Tempest's cause.  And does that hurt very much? No (Rhetorical).  Bob Dylan is the man - and yes, he can sing.  More importantly, he can write - song write! Highly recommended 35th album by `the man.', clocking in still at over seven minutes. 0307887448|3.0|Ready Player one is a fun book, that has its flaws, but is still worth the read.<br /><br />Full of 80's geek pop culture references, it appeals to a very nitch set of readers but creates a exciting, enjoyable story. The premise, while hardly going to any new territory with its future of poverty and energy shortages, is presented in a spirited and well written way. There are enough twists in the story to keep it from becoming stale, and the idea of a grand treasure hunt in a virtual reality gives it a feel worthy of the best adventure stories.<br /><br />What really sells the story though, is the characters. Wade is a likeable protagonist, who still has some glaring flaws (he can be impatient, arrogant, and is obviously afraid of dealing with the real world.) His competition in the hunt prove to be well rounded characters as well. Art3mis is flighty, fun and brilliant, but with a terrible self-esteem that mars her interactions with others.  Shoto and Daito want to come off as cool and composed samurai warriors, but are in reality far from those ideals. And H has the most to hide, under his confident avatar. Still, you come to care deeply for all these characters, particularly in the second half of the book when the story widens from Wade.<br /><br />The flaws however keep this from being the geeky masterpiece it could have been. To start with, the lists of Geek references gets old fast. And having to explain the references as well. If I don't know what the Breakfast club is, I'm not going to get the joke any better when you explain it. And if I already know what it is, its annoying to have to sit through the explanation.  When Wade keeps saying things like \\"I read all the Cannon: Douglas Adams, Terry Pratchet, Terry Brooks, Peter Beagle, Peter David...\\" Etc. you find yourself skimming ahead of the laundry list of \\"Oh! These are cool geek things that exist!\\"<br /><br />The author also takes the safe path far more than he should.  Wade has far too much luck in the real world. His dealings with the main villain, an evil corporation willing to kill to win the contest tend to build up tension only to be resolved easily by having Wade have just the right skill, or some other deus ex machina solution. That kind of thing can be forgiven occasionally (his luck being in his hide out at the right time, or the reveal on what the magic quarter does were well set up ahead of time, and so are acceptable) but the idea that this kid just happens to get the right information to be a genius hacker, and that this that solves all his problems, is just a little too much to swallow.  I was willing to look past it to get to the end, but it took away from the really brilliant moments of the story.<br /><br />He also takes the easy way out with Wade's dealings in the real world. The big reveal about Art3mis is easy. Why not make her something challenging that really would tests Wade's love of her mind before he meets her body? Or why not use H's big reveal to challenge that relationship. The convenient truth about H immediately takes him out of being a contender for Art3mis, and doesn't change anything about his relationship with Wade. The whole point of the real world meeting with his online fellowship is to challenge his pre-conceived ideas about them. Instead, he gets off easy.<br /><br />Again, its not a terrible book at all. Its fun, and I loved the characters. But it had the feel of someone writing a really fun role playing game module, but failing to flesh it out enough to make a really great novel. Still its worth the read, and not at all hard to get through. I enjoyed it. I just think it had greater potential.

B00004SVID|4.0|First of all as this is a live recording, \\"Is There Anybody Out There? The Wall Live 1980-1981\\" lacks the distinct harshness of the original studio album, and its overall impression is less striking and memorable. However that does not mean that this version isn't worth checking out, or even buying.<br /><br />The Wall was such an ambitious project that several songs were cut down for the LP: this is no secret and fans know it. Having the full versions of these songs--Another Brick in the Wall, Mother, Empty Spaces/What Shall We Do Now, Young Lust, The Show Must Go On, and Outside the Wall--is reason enough to purchase this live album. But there are other reasons. A few tracks, like Goodbye Blue Sky and Nobody Home, actually sound a bit better, and the theatrical aspects of Waters' idea (which do not come across in the studio album) are adequately captured.<br /><br />Furthermore Richard Wright, who was forced out of the band and was mostly in the background during the studio sessions, really shines on this performance. On songs where Bob Ezrin or studio musicians did keyboards on the original, Rick does it all. The result is more interesting and artful than any of the piano and organ parts on the original album. Despite not being a full member of the band in the early eighties, Rick seems to be enthusiastic and engaged during the concerts. I find that a welcome change over his lethargy and absence in the studio. If you are a big fan of Rick like I am and you feel that The Wall could have used more of his touch, definitely give this a listen; it's a bit different.<br /><br />All in all this is a collector's item, only really for fans who are obsessed with The Wall and its live show. I was fortunate enough to see Waters play it earlier this year, and there are some differences between how he does it now and how he did it then. Today he's a calmer and more accommodating man, an entertainer who identifies with his audience. During his time in the Floyd he was none of that. You can totally feel his tension in these 1980 recordings from Earl's Court. The songs are played a little faster than both the studio album and the recent live show, as there's a sense of urgency and perhaps even despair. It's a remarkable thing. If you like Pink Floyd and Roger Waters' masterwork The Wall, there's no reason not to enjoy this unique experience.

Lola is a true San Fran individual with an explosive personality and a huge problem: her dad's don't like her twenty-year-old boyfriend and her ex-friend's family moved back in next door. Life was going so well for Lola. This story is truly a girl and boy story in the sense that as a girl you can relate and as a boy you learn and understand. The story is strong and keeps you interested most of the time. There are slow points but that's for every book. It's not a quick read and it's not a slow read. As a boy I really enjoyed it and suggest it to other boys. They will learn and understand. Also, if you're not into such things, this book does talk about sex and has appropriate romance situations. READ THIS!

I'm writing this mainly because of what's NOT discernable by evaluating the sample tracks.<br /><br />When John's got everything working, the understated vocals and meaningful lyrics are driven along by his underrated guitar work.  This album is well written and well sung, but he never lets his guitar take lead, preferring to drift along playing rhythm.<br /><br />The first 2 plays through I listened expectantly for him to cut in and take the song over - he never does and it's a shame.  Lacking that drive from either vocals, percussion, or guitar to give focus, it floats along listlessly rather than forging ahead.<br /><br />All in all, it's certainly not bad but it falls to the background far too easily to be fulfilling for me as an active listener when I find myself thinking about other things rather than the music.  Marc Knopfler would be a good example of what John could do if he strikes the right balance making a more powerful, memorable record.
